He Built The Revenue Engines for Google Facebook Square

Invest Like The Best(38.0K) ·76:30 ·21.3K views · 2026-01-29
Mark Zuckerberg Eric Schmidt OpenAI Google Keynote
The AI revolution is profoundly reshaping product development, demanding a new breed of hands-on product managers focused on judgment and customer outcomes.
The AI revolution is profoundly reshaping product development, demanding a new breed of hands-on product managers focused on judgment and customer outcomes. Businesses must adapt by building durable, full-stack AI-native solutions and leaders must foster cultures of deep...

TL;DR

The AI revolution is profoundly reshaping product development, demanding a new breed of hands-on product managers focused on judgment and customer outcomes. Businesses must adapt by building durable, full-stack AI-native solutions and leaders must foster cultures of deep expertise and efficient communication.

The AI Revolution in Product Development

  • AI is fundamentally reshaping product development roles and processes:
      • Traditional, clearly defined roles (PMs articulate, designers design, engineers build) are dissolving.
      • The emergence of resilient, "long-running" AI agents means non-technical individuals can rapidly build functional tools (e.g., a video transcription tool in one hour, vs. failing six months prior).
  • Product development is becoming "bottoms-up" and highly fluid due to rapid capability changes:
      • Strict, upfront product specifications are obsolete; the focus shifts to high-level customer needs ("the why").
      • Product managers are now hands-on, prototyping and even checking code into production repositories alongside engineers and researchers.
      • AI tools are expected to review code themselves soon, further automating development stages.
  • Design roles are merging with product management, shifting demand towards engineering talent:
      • Many companies prioritize hiring additional engineers over designers, as AI can leverage existing design systems to generate designs.
      • The engineer-to-PM/designer ratio is evolving from 1:3 or 1:10 to 1:20.
  • Software is increasingly non-deterministic, necessitating new evaluation techniques:
      • Slight input variations can lead to completely different outputs, unlike the predictable software of the past.
      • Product managers (and researchers) now own "evals"—evaluating AI-generated outputs, often by writing AI to assess the results, as human evaluation alone is insufficient.

Strategic Product Philosophy for the AI Era

  • Product managers must balance customer and business needs by focusing on measurable outcomes:
      • The PM's core role is to be the "keeper of the why," understanding customer pain points and how solutions add company value.
      • Success is defined by "outcomes," which are articulated as specific customer behavior changes (e.g., "customers will go from doing X to doing Y").
      • Every feature launch requires a clear hypothesis grounded in anticipated customer behavior change.
  • Judgment is the most future-proof human skill amidst "AI slop":
      • In an era of immense AI-driven productivity, where AI engines produce vast amounts of code and content, human judgment is paramount to discern what truly matters and what should be built.
      • This judgment applies to product strategy, code quality, security, and design coherence.
  • Building durable AI applications requires tackling deep problems with custom data and aiming for full system replacement:
      • Focus on high-value workflows within industries where highly paid, repetitive human roles can be automated by agentic AI (e.g., designers, architects, lawyers).
      • Avoid "light" solutions or generic agent builders, as foundational AI model companies (e.g., Gemini Agent Builder, ChatGPT Enterprise) can easily replicate them.
      • Durability stems from owning scarce assets (licenses, unique data), control points, hardware, essential workflows, or network effects.

Evolving Business Models & Market Dynamics

  • AI-native companies must aim to replace entire "systems of record" rather than merely building "systems of action" atop existing platforms:
      • Legacy systems (Salesforce, Epic) are cutting off API access to AI agent companies that threatened to devalue their core data.
      • New AI companies are forced to build their own full platforms, including the system of record, making "system of action" strategies unviable without ownership.
      • Competing with incumbents requires significant investment in migration tools (e.g., a two-year effort for complex data sets like Salesforce).
  • Older software companies face significant threats, particularly those with utility-based pricing:
      • Companies charging per "seat" (e.g., Zendesk) are vulnerable as AI agents can siphon off utility, allowing customers to reduce paid seats. These companies may need to go private to fundamentally transform their pricing to outcome-based models.
      • Companies with high "data half-life" (e.g., ERPs like NetSuite, or Salesforce for customer records) are more insulated, as ripping out core systems carries significant career risk for customers, and these incumbents can bundle AI agents atop their existing data.
  • Advertising in the AI era relies on three core models:

1. Owning a coveted group of users and a primary interaction surface: (e.g., Google Search, Facebook/Meta for identity, ChatGPT for intent + identity).

2. Driving specific, measurable outcomes for advertisers: (e.g., AppLoving for mobile app installs).

3. Being an exclusive provider for a large advertiser or demand source: (e.g., The Trade Desk for large brand display budgets).

  • Unsuccessful advertising models include acting as a middleman on top of dominant platforms:
      • Building ad optimization businesses on Google, Facebook, or OpenAI's ecosystems is unsustainable, as these platforms will inevitably integrate successful capabilities.

Leadership and Talent in the New Landscape

  • Great leaders possess a "superpower" intrinsically aligned with their company's core mission:
      • Larry & Sergey (Google): Technological superiority and scale (e.g., Gmail's 1GB storage, AdSense aiming to serve all internet ads).
      • Mark Zuckerberg (Facebook): Growth and engagement in consumer products, learning by shadowing to develop foundational ideas (e.g., Custom Audiences).
      • Jack Dorsey (Square): Design thinking focused on extreme friction removal and self-serve experience (e.g., Square POS, shifting risk to transaction level).
  • Effective leadership communication is clear, repetitive, and transparent:
      • Eric Schmidt used images-only presentations to convey strategy, focusing on emotional resonance over words.
      • Weekly CEO emails should feature "Top of Mind" (product, business, team), "Performance Update," and "Miscellaneous," with repetition to ensure key messages sink in.
      • Candid transparency in communication, especially about challenges, fosters input and ownership from talented team members.
  • Hiring emphasizes "doers" and "builders" capable of orchestrating AI agents:
      • The future workforce will increasingly consist of individual contributors (ICs) who manage "armies of AI agents" to perform functions.
      • Hiring processes should prioritize "work projects" over traditional interviews, assessing actual building and problem-solving skills (e.g., coding, market analysis for acquisitions, customer acquisition challenges).
      • Candidates should demonstrate agency by questioning premises and proposing alternative solutions, rather than just executing given tasks.
  • Career success in the AI era demands long-term impact and authentic engagement:
      • Staying in roles long enough (minimum 3-4 years) to achieve significant impact is crucial; frequent "job hopping" (12-18 months) is a major red flag for hiring managers.
      • Founders need "authenticity"—a genuine, lived experience or deep curiosity driving their problem choice, rather than just wanting to start a company.
      • Successful founders rigorously navigate the "idea maze," understanding historical approaches and justifying their chosen solution against alternatives.

Like what advice would you give someone that's trying to pick the thing around which the company is going to optimize? >> Yeah, the northstar metric is a is a metric that is an indicator of company growth and customer value. So it actually balances customer value and business value nicely. Nostra metrics in my opinion should not be revenue. It should be something that is directly correlated with customer value. So for example, if customers are doing well, the Nostra metric should go up and to the right, but it should also lead in business the business doing well. For example, for Square, the Nostar metric was GPV, which is volume of payments processed. It was not correlated to re it was somewhat correlated to revenue, but it most importantly showed that the number of the amount of payment processed to the company was continuing to grow. At Facebook, the northstar metric was DA us. It was actually monthly active users. Then it over time went to daily active users because it was a sense it was an indication of how engaged C users were. Now, one of the most important things about an NSM is that it needs to be coupled with what we call check metrics. In other words, NSTAR metrics if they're left alone can as you know incentives drive behavior. So if you tell a team go and optimize this Nstra metric they will do what it is going to go up 100%. But then many things that you don't want to go down could go down. So for example in in the Door Dash case you could say I want to grow GMV which is the gross merchandising value which is the Nordstar metric. Now GMV is the total order of total value of all the orders that go through the marketplace. I could make it grow up by offering by setting delivery fee to zero by setting everything to zero and what happens then? The company's revenue goes to zero. So you basically want a check metric that is maybe a check metric around the health of the customer and a check metric around the health of the company that basically hold this that are the guard rails around this Nstra metric. So in the case of Door Dash it might be I want to maintain a certain gross margin percentage or I want to maintain a certain customer retention percentage something like that. Marc Andreessen The real AI boom hasnt even started yet

Lenny's Podcast(535.0K) ·104:36 ·151.3K views · 2026-01-29
Marc Andreessen Howard Marks OpenAI a16z Keynote
AI marks a profoundly historic juncture, arriving precisely when humanity needs it to counteract decades of slow productivity growth and declining populations.
AI marks a profoundly historic juncture, arriving precisely when humanity needs it to counteract decades of slow productivity growth and declining populations. Rather than displacing workers, AI will elevate human capabilities, transform job tasks, and drive unprecedented...

TL;DR

AI marks a profoundly historic juncture, arriving precisely when humanity needs it to counteract decades of slow productivity growth and declining populations. Rather than displacing workers, AI will elevate human capabilities, transform job tasks, and drive unprecedented economic growth, demanding individuals to become "super-empowered specialists" across multiple domains.

A Historic Moment for Humanity

  • AI is the "philosopher's stone" of our era, transforming the most common resource (sand) into the most rare (thought), marking a fundamentally new technological capability.
  • We are living through a "very, very historic time," comparable in magnitude to the fall of the Berlin Wall in 1989 or the end of World War II.
  • Three mega-trends are colliding:
      • Collapse of trust in legacy institutions globally.
      • Liberation of national and global discourse, expanding freedom of speech and thought.
      • Massive geopolitical shifts across the US, Europe, China, and Latin America.
  • AI's reasoning capabilities are proving effective: What began as creative applications (composing Shakespeare) has evolved into verifiable problem-solving in critical domains like medicine, science, and law.
      • AI is developing new math theorems.
      • Leading programmers observed AI coding better than humans as early as the past holiday season.

Economic and Demographic Dynamics

  • AI arrives at a critical juncture: It is precisely what is needed to address two underlying, poorly understood economic realities:
      • Decades of slow technological progress: Productivity growth in the US has been remarkably low for the last 50 years, running at half the pace of 1940-1970 and one-third the pace of 1870-1940.
      • Global demographic collapse: Reproduction rates are declining below replacement levels (e.g., under 2 in the US and China), leading to projected depopulation over the next century.
  • AI is a necessary solution, not a threat, to jobs:
      • AI will increase productivity, stimulating economic growth and innovation, which will create a "wash with opportunity" for new fields and jobs, similar to the 1870-1930 era.
      • Human workers will be at a premium: Declining populations combined with potential decreases in immigration will make remaining human workers more valuable.
      • Concerns about "job loss" are reductive; the focus should be on "task loss" and task redefinition within existing jobs (e.g., executives doing their own typing, secretaries shifting tasks).
  • Economic benefits will be widespread: Massive productivity growth will lead to:
      • Collapsing prices (deflation) for goods and services, which is equivalent to a "gigantic increase in wealth" for everyone.
      • Easier funding for social safety nets, as the cost of healthcare, housing, and education would significantly decrease.

The Transformation of Roles and Skills

  • The "Mexican Standoff" among PMs, Engineers, and Designers: Each role now believes AI allows them to perform tasks traditionally done by the other two (e.g., coders believing they can also be PMs and designers). Andreessen notes, "they're actually all kind of correct."
  • Rise of the "super-empowered individual": AI will enable good performers to become "very good" and great performers to become "spectacularly great."
      • This requires individuals to expand laterally, mastering not just their core domain but also becoming proficient in related areas using AI tools.
      • Combining skills creates exponential value: Being good at two things is "more than double" the value; three things is "more than triple," creating a "super relevant specialist" (the "E" or "F" framework).
  • Continuous learning is paramount: Individuals should actively use AI to "train me up" in new skills, seeking feedback, and understanding the underlying mechanisms of AI-generated work.
      • The nature of coding has continually evolved from human "calculators" to machine code, assembly, higher-level languages (C), scripting (Perl, Python), and now AI orchestrating "code bots."
      • Designers will shift from routine tasks (icon creation) to "capital D design," focusing on higher-level questions of function, human experience, and emotional impact.
  • Understanding the "why" remains critical: Even as AI automates tasks, humans must understand *what* the AI is doing, *why* it's doing it, and *how* to guide it effectively (e.g., debugging AI-generated code).

AI's Broader Societal & Business Implications

  • AI revolutionizes education: AI makes one-on-one tutoring economically feasible for the first time, a method historically proven to drastically improve learning outcomes (Bloom's 2 Sigma Effect).
      • This will foster a hybrid approach of traditional schooling augmented by AI-powered, personalized learning.
      • AI empowers "agency" in children, allowing them to lead, create, and be primary contributors to the world in a profoundly new way.
  • Challenges remain in "atoms" industries: While "bits" (software) have seen rapid progress, "atoms" (physical infrastructure, healthcare) face structural impediments like red tape, bureaucracy, cartels, and monopolies that resist change.
      • AI's presence might force a re-evaluation of these assumptions, leading to an "it's time to build" moment in these stagnant sectors.
  • The future of companies could be radically different:
      • Beyond redefining products, AI could transform job structures within companies and even the definition of a company itself.
      • The "one-person billion-dollar outcome" could become more common, with founders orchestrating an "army of AI bots."
      • Autonomous AI agents on blockchains could potentially function as self-sustaining businesses.
  • AI will exceed human capabilities: Andreessen views the concept of "Artificial General Intelligence" (AGI) as simply matching human performance as an understatement.
      • Human IQ caps around 160; AI will quickly surpass this, reaching IQs of 180, 200, 250, 300.
      • AI coders, doctors, and lawyers will ultimately outperform their human counterparts. The "human equivalent" will be a mere footnote in AI's rapid advancement.

Navigating Uncertainty and Moats in AI

  • Massive technological shifts are unpredictable: Initial confident predictions about industry structure, market winners, and product killer apps are often wrong due to cascading structural changes.
  • AI moats are currently unclear:
      • AI models (LLMs): While requiring massive investment, there's a risk of rapid commoditization. OpenAI's GPT-3 was replicated by five other American companies, five Chinese companies, and open-source alternatives within 1-1.5 years.
      • AI applications (apps): The value could shift from the models themselves to the applications that adapt these models for specific, domain-centric functions (e.g., medical or legal AI apps).
  • Strategy of "Indeterminate Optimism": Given the vast unknowns, the optimal approach for venture capital firms like A16Z is to:
      • Fund a broad portfolio of "determinate optimists"—founders with specific, ambitious plans.
      • Run as many experiments as possible, fostering an ecosystem of smart people doing interesting things.
      • Maintain flexibility and adaptability rather than making rigid forecasts.

Notable Quotes

"AI is the philosopher stone. Now we have a technology that transfers the most common thing in the world which is sand converted into the most rare thing in the world which is thought." - on AI's fundamental breakthrough "The remaining human workers are going to be at a premium, not at a discount." - on AI's impact on human labor in the face of demographic decline "The additive effect of being good at two things is more than double. The additive effect of being good at three things is more than triple. You become a super relevant specialist in the combination of the domains." - on skill development and career strategy in the AI era "Human equivalent is just going to be like a footnote... What do we get to do in a world in which we actually have machines that are better than that?" - on AI exceeding human capabilities beyond AGI

Marc Andreessen This is the most important era in tech history heres why

a16z(245.0K) ·91:05 ·63.4K views · 2026-01-29
Marc Andreessen Anthropic Keynote
AI represents a profoundly historic shift, acting as a "philosopher's stone" that can convert common resources into advanced thought, arriving providentially as global population declines and technological progress has stagnated.
AI represents a profoundly historic shift, acting as a "philosopher's stone" that can convert common resources into advanced thought, arriving providentially as global population declines and technological progress has stagnated. This transformation will redefine products, jobs,...

TL;DR

AI represents a profoundly historic shift, acting as a "philosopher's stone" that can convert common resources into advanced thought, arriving providentially as global population declines and technological progress has stagnated. This transformation will redefine products, jobs, and company structures, placing a premium on "superpowered" individuals who master AI to combine deep expertise with broad, cross-functional skills.

AI's Historical Significance & Macro-Economic Context

  • We are in a profoundly historic era, with AI as a central transformative force.
      • AI is likened to a "philosopher's stone," converting common resources (sand) into rare and valuable thought.
      • This moment is comparable in magnitude to the fall of the Berlin Wall or the end of World War II, driven by a collision of factors: a collapse of trust in institutions, a liberated global conversation, massive geopolitical shifts, and the emergence of AI.
  • AI's arrival is serendipitously timed to address critical global challenges.
      • For the past 50 years, the economy has experienced very slow technological change, with productivity growth running at half the pace of 1940-1970 and a third of 1870-1940 levels.
      • Simultaneously, there is a global demographic collapse, with human reproduction rates declining below replacement levels in many countries, including the U.S. and China, leading to projected depopulation.
      • AI is necessary to drive productivity and economic growth, preventing stagnation and filling jobs that a shrinking human population will no longer be able to do.
  • Concerns about mass job loss are likely overblown due to unique macro-economic factors.
      • Even if AI triples productivity growth, it would only restore job churn to levels seen between 1870-1930, an era widely perceived as full of opportunity and innovation.
      • The combination of declining population and likely reduced immigration means remaining human workers will be "at a premium, not at a discount."
      • A future of massive AI-driven productivity growth would lead to a "collapse in prices" for goods and services, acting as a "gigantic increase in wealth" for everyone and simplifying the funding of social safety nets.
  • However, structural impediments exist that may slow AI's full societal impact, particularly in "atoms" rather than "bits."
      • Bureaucratic processes, red tape, politics, unions, cartels, and monopolies (e.g., in healthcare) historically prevent rapid change.
      • AI might force a re-evaluation of these assumptions, but widespread, overnight transformation across all sectors is unlikely due to these deep-seated structures.

The Evolving Workforce: Individual Skills & Roles

  • AI is creating a "Mexican standoff" among product managers, engineers, and designers, enabling individuals to perform multiple roles.
      • Each role now believes they can execute tasks of the other two with AI assistance (e.g., coders can PM and design, PMs can code and design).
      • This belief is "all kind of correct," as AI is increasingly adept at tasks within all three domains.
  • The focus should shift from job loss to "task loss," as roles evolve rather than disappear.
      • Jobs are bundles of tasks; AI will change the nature of these tasks (e.g., executives now do their own typing, while assistants handle other responsibilities).
      • Individuals must adapt by swapping out old tasks for new, AI-leveraged ones.
  • AI empowers "superpowered individuals" who can achieve exponentially greater productivity.
      • Individuals who are "good" become "very good"; those who are "great" become "spectacularly great" (e.g., 10x engineers).
      • The "additive effect of being good at two things is more than double, three things is more than triple." This creates "super relevant specialists" who combine deep expertise in one domain with AI-augmented proficiency in others (e.g., an engineer deeply skilled in coding, but also able to leverage AI for design and product management).
  • Continuous learning and direct engagement with AI are critical for career development.
      • Individuals should spend "every spare hour" using AI to "train me up" in new skills, ask for assignments, and evaluate results.
      • Even with AI generating code, deep understanding of underlying principles remains crucial to orchestrate AI, debug its outputs, and provide effective feedback.
      • AI serves as a powerful, always-available tutor, capable of explaining concepts, quizzing users, and critiquing work.

Founders, Company Building, and Moats in the AI Era

  • Leading-edge founders are exploring AI's impact across three levels of company evolution.

1. Redefining Products: Determining if AI is merely an additive feature to existing products or if it entirely reinvents product categories and industries, leading to new market leaders.

2. Changing Jobs: Leveraging AI to radically increase team productivity, potentially reducing headcount (e.g., needing 10 "superpowered" coders instead of 100) or enabling existing teams to achieve 10x more output.

3. Redefining the Company Itself: Pursuing the "one person billion-dollar outcome" where a founder oversees an "army of AI bots," or even envisioning autonomous AI entities functioning as businesses (e.g., AI bots on the blockchain generating dividends).

  • The landscape of competitive moats in AI remains highly uncertain and rapidly evolving.
      • Initial assumptions of strong moats for large AI models (due to immense cost, compute, and talent) have been challenged by rapid commoditization.
      • Within 1-1.5 years of GPT-3's release, multiple other American and Chinese companies, along with open-source alternatives, developed comparable capabilities.
      • There's ongoing debate whether value will concentrate in foundational AI models or shift to AI-powered applications that tailor models for specific domains (e.g., Claude Code writing Co-work in 1.5 weeks highlights both AI's power and potential low barrier to entry for apps).
      • The history of AI breakthroughs over the last three years has been everything that looks like it's the fundamental breakthrough gets basically replicated and lapped very quickly.
  • A flexible, "indeterminate optimist" approach is necessary for investing in this dynamic environment.
      • Given the vast number of unknowns across technology, regulation, politics, and global dynamics, making definitive predictions about future industry structure or market winners is difficult.
      • The most effective strategy involves supporting many "determinate optimist" founders with specific plans, running numerous experiments, and embracing surprises in a "complex adaptive system."

Beyond Human Intelligence: The Future of AI

  • The concept of AGI (Artificial General Intelligence) will likely be a "footnote" as AI quickly surpasses human intellectual capabilities.
      • The "prosaic" definition of AGI (AI performing all economically relevant tasks as well as a human) may soon be met or already be close.
      • However, human skill and IQ are capped by biological limitations (e.g., human IQ tops out around 160, with most experts around 130-140).
      • AI models are expected to quickly exceed human IQ levels (e.g., 160, 180, 200, 250, 300+) and performance in tasks.
  • The world will soon experience machines that are "better than human" in various domains.
      • Examples include AI coders outperforming the best human coders, AI doctors surpassing top human physicians, and AI lawyers exceeding human legal experts.
      • This development should be seen as overwhelmingly positive, akin to having more "Einsteins" in the world.
  • We are transitioning from a world capped by human biology to one where machines provide capabilities far beyond our inherent limits.
      • This opens up an "exploratory process" for actually exceeding human capability, making the moment AI achieves "human equivalence" a mere "footnote."

TL;DR

Project Genie, powered by Genie 3, is launching to Google US Ultra subscribers, enabling users to generate and interact in real-time, custom 3D worlds. This interactive world model represents a significant step beyond passive video generation, with immense potential for entertainment, education, and embodied AI.

Project Genie: Interactive World Models for All

  • Project Genie is an interactive web application, launching today for Google US Ultra subscribers.
      • Built in partnership with Google Labs and Creative Lab.
      • Aims to be for interactive 3D worlds what Flow is for video (VO).
  • Genie 3, the underlying technology, is an advanced "world model."
      • Unlike video models that generate short, passive videos from text, Genie 3 generates interactive environments frame-by-frame.
      • Users can actively navigate and influence the world in real-time (e.g., move left, right, jump).
      • The goal is to simulate a "world" that users can feel present in and interact with, bringing generative AI to interactive media.
      • Draws inspiration from reinforcement learning's use of world models for AI agents, but now applies generative AI for compelling human experiences.

World Creation and Dynamic Interaction

  • Users can easily define and customize their worlds and characters.
      • World creation starts with a qualitative text prompt (e.g., "coral reef," "goldfish").
      • Leverages Nano Banana Pro to generate an initial 2D canvas/image, which can then be transformed into a 3D world.
      • Offers additional controls, including uploading personal photos to bring characters (like "Bob the Nanobanana Dinosaur") or objects to life within the world.
  • The model creates responsive and dynamic environments.
      • Users control a character within the generated world (e.g., a fish, a dinosaur in a library).
      • The environment implicitly learns and responds to character actions, exhibiting basic physics (e.g., water splashing, balls rolling).
      • The transition from a 2D image to an explorable 3D world is a key "wow moment" for testers.
  • While interactive, it's not currently a game with complex narratives or adversaries.
      • Current focus is on navigation and basic interaction; complex narratives are a future possibility.
  • A curated gallery provides inspiration and starting points.
      • Helps overcome the "blank prompt problem" for new users, offering diverse examples to build upon or remix.

Technical Achievements and Ongoing Development

  • Achieving real-time, low-latency interaction was a core challenge.
      • Genie 3 delivers real-time responsiveness for user actions, a significant leap from Genie 2 (which was 10 seconds, lower resolution, and not real-time).
      • This requires maintaining consistency from frame to frame with unknown future inputs, a harder problem than passive video generation.
  • Balancing capability, speed, and cost is a continuous effort.
      • Worlds currently last 60 seconds, a trade-off to manage serving costs and maintain dynamism, which can gradually reduce over longer periods.
      • Ongoing research aims to improve capability, speed, and reduce cost, enabling broader access and longer, richer experiences.
  • Development involved extensive cross-functional collaboration.
      • A sprint since the August model announcement, bringing together teams from DeepMind, Google Labs, Creative Lab, infrastructure, and communications.
      • The journey from a research project (Genie 1 paper) to a public experience highlights Google's "research to reality" capability.
  • A trusted tester program provided crucial insights.
      • Feedback from creatives, educators, and diverse users informed the model's development and identified key use cases and future directions.

Vision for Future Impact and Use Cases

  • Project Genie is a starting point for evolving interactive media.
      • Future iterations will focus on expanding controls, app scaffolding, and exploring new surfaces beyond the current web app (e.g., developer APIs).
      • The vision is to make passive media consumption fully interactive, creating truly bespoke narratives.
  • Significant potential exists across various domains:
      • Education & Entertainment: Personalized experiences, such as allowing users to overcome fears (e.g., walk in a room full of spiders) or relive childhood memories in interactive environments.
      • Embodied Intelligence & Robotics: Training AI agents (like Simmer, a Gemini-powered agent) in diverse, user-generated 3D worlds, and potentially enabling real-time decision-making for robots.
      • Creative Challenges: Users can create and share basic challenges within worlds (e.g., "fly through these rings").
  • Long-term aspiration is to create indistinguishable-from-reality simulations.
      • The ultimate "North Star" is to have "a copy of the universe" that allows for limitless interaction and experimentation, including integrating and augmenting real-world spaces.
  • User experimentation is encouraged despite current limitations.
      • While prompting may not always be robust, early adopters are encouraged to persist and explore, as unexpected and interesting results can emerge from this frontier technology.

Results 2025 Norges Bank Investment Management

Norges Bank Investment Management(102.0K) ·52:21 ·3.8K views · 2026-01-29
Google Meta Earnings
The fund achieved strong 15% returns in 2025, reaching over 25,000 billion NOK, primarily driven by exceptional performance in technology (AI) and commodities.
The fund achieved strong 15% returns in 2025, reaching over 25,000 billion NOK, primarily driven by exceptional performance in technology (AI) and commodities. Despite this success, the fund acknowledges significant geopolitical and economic turbulence, leading to increased...

TL;DR

The fund achieved strong 15% returns in 2025, reaching over 25,000 billion NOK, primarily driven by exceptional performance in technology (AI) and commodities. Despite this success, the fund acknowledges significant geopolitical and economic turbulence, leading to increased focus on stress testing, diversification, and managing concentration risks associated with dominant tech holdings.

2025 Fund Performance and Overview

  • Record Fund Size and Returns:
      • Fund stood at over 25,000 billion NOK by year-end 2025.
      • Delivered a 15% return, marking the third consecutive year of double-digit returns.
      • Monetary value of returns exceeded 2,300 billion NOK.
      • Net inflows totaled 327 billion NOK, slightly less than previous three years.
  • Relative Performance Slightly Below Benchmark:
      • Overall relative return was negative 28 basis points.
      • Equities posted a positive 7 basis points relative return.
      • Fixed income had a very strong year with positive numbers.
      • Real assets (real estate) showed negative relative performance due to flat asset values measured against rising stock and bond markets.
      • Allocation decisions, including an underweight in equities, contributed negative 8 basis points.
  • Currency Impact: A stronger Norwegian Kroner (NOK) weakened the fund's value when measured in local currency.

Key Market Drivers and Sector Performance

  • Technology Dominance, Driven by AI:
      • Equity markets (70% of the portfolio) contributed significantly with 19% returns.
      • Tech sector gained over 20% and drove nearly 60% of total market returns.
      • AI Acceleration and Volatility: Deepseek chatbot emergence led to a sell-off questioning Western AI dominance; "Liberation Day" tariffs in April caused another dip before a strong recovery into AI infrastructure.
      • Concentration Risk: Top 10 equity holdings now constitute 20% of the portfolio, the highest level ever, raising concerns about concentration risk.
      • Winners and Losers: Semiconductor companies (tight supply) and Alphabet (Gemini 3 launch) were clear winners; some software companies underperformed as AI's business model impact was debated.
      • AI Adoption: Over half of Fortune 500 companies use Cursor (AI code editor); ChatGPT serves over 800 million users weekly.
  • Commodities Comeback:
      • Metals (gold, silver, copper) saw over 40% returns, outperforming digital assets.
      • Drivers: Inflation eroding trust in traditional currencies (central bank gold purchases amid geopolitical uncertainty), fears of critical metal shortages (copper supply issues, China's rare earth control), and massive demand from AI infrastructure (data centers, chips).
  • Strong Financial Sector:
      • Financials delivered 30% returns, with European banks outperforming the "Magnificent Seven" over the last five years.
      • Factors: Sustained economic growth fueling loan demand, a booming Wall Street (volatility creating trading opportunities, corporate optimism driving M&A), and aligned regulatory/political support for economic growth.
      • Outlook: The team is taking less risk, rotating into less favored financial segments, as market expectations for continued bank earnings growth are high.

Geopolitical and Macroeconomic Risks

  • Comprehensive Stress Tests: Four realistic scenarios were modeled for potential severe impacts on the fund:
      • AI Correction: A broad market sell-off if expected AI productivity gains do not materialize.
      • Fragmented World: Global division into blocks, leading to reduced trade, increased inflation, and lower growth (modeled impact of up to 37% on the portfolio).
      • Regional Debt Crisis: Investor loss of confidence in governments' ability to service high debt levels, leading to capital flight.
      • Extreme Weather: Impact of droughts, floods, and storms on crops, translating to lower growth and higher inflation.
  • Geopolitical Tensions and Diversification:
      • Ongoing global conflicts (Ukraine, Israel-Hamas, US-Venezuela tensions) highlight a turbulent backdrop.
      • An expert council reported on concerns regarding high US exposure in the fund's portfolio due to geopolitical risks.
      • A work plan is underway to explore addressing regional diversification, particularly concerning US holdings, beyond benchmark rules.
  • AI Bubble Concerns:
      • Difficulty in gauging if an "AI bubble" exists, despite the fund "benefiting in a tremendous way."
      • New phenomena include AI companies taking on debt and "circularity in some structures."
      • The fund cannot simply divest from AI companies due to its mandate and significant portfolio concentration; broader diversification strategies are under review.

Asset Class Strategies and Outlook

  • Fixed Income Resilience (27% of portfolio):
      • Achieved 5% returns despite market shocks (tariffs, AI, geopolitics, fiscal concerns).
      • Central banks cut short-term rates, but long-term yield curves deepened due to increased uncertainty, shifts in investor preference, and anticipated government bond issuance.
      • German 10-year yields ended slightly higher despite ECB rate cuts, driven by expectations of increased government spending.
  • Real Estate Stability and New Strategy:
      • Overall "big fat zero" return (unlisted +4%, listed -4%) after several turbulent years.
      • Data centers were the only "hot" area; general sentiment remained muted.
      • Outlook: Cautiously optimistic, expecting occupational fundamentals (rising rents, subdued new construction) to improve in 2026. A new strategy aims to capitalize on motivated sellers post-turbulence.
  • Growing Renewable Infrastructure (Smallest Portfolio Segment):
      • Achieved 18% returns in 2025.
      • Drivers: Global demand for electricity (AI, data centers, EVs, heat pumps) is driving electrification, with wind and solar being the cheapest and fastest solutions.
      • Increased Investment: Over 100 billion NOK invested in unlisted renewable energy in 2025, including partnerships for offshore wind in Europe (RWE) and a 22% stake in Germany's largest Transmission System Operator (Tenner Germany).
      • Strategy: Focus on robust returns, proven technologies, stable countries, and essential infrastructure; sees less competition and a "discount" for "green" investments compared to earlier "hot market" premiums.

Notable Quotes

You can't print more copper or gold." - on the appeal of hard assets amidst market volatility "The market isn't without risks, and we're bracing ourselves for yet another volatile year in tech, where debates around potential bubbles, profitability, power constraints, and competition with China will continue to dominate the conversation." - on the future outlook for the technology sector "Now it's a dis it seems like it's a discount because things are green, right? And so they are just much more attractive and we are in a unique situation that we can make big investments..." - on the current investment opportunity in renewable infrastructure

But in 2025, the metals in the periodic table made a very strong comeback. There are three main drivers behind this 40% plus return. First, inflation has eroded trust in traditional currencies. Hard assets such as gold have been climbing for years, but 2025 was exceptional with gold stocks rallying over 150% as central banks bought aggressively amid geopolitical uncertainties, wars, territorial ambitions, and trade disputes. The gold rally also spread to silver and other precious metals. Second, the world fears that we are running short on critical metals. Copper mines around the world have been having supply issues. Meanwhile, China controls most rare earth and downstream refining. So government started asking do we have secure supply and the prices reflected this concern. Finally, AI needs physical infrastructure. Data centers and AI chips require enormous amounts of copper and other junior metals. This is adding to the fear around the demand supply balances. So in summary, in uncertain times, investors and speculators turn to what is tangible and scarce. You can't print more copper or gold. And this fear and scarcity proved quite valuable in 2025. >> Thank you so much uh Arnab that's uh very interesting. We move on to technology and we will hear from our portfolio manager Karina Sucha who is a portfolio manager in uh hardware technology. Karina, are you with us? Absolutely. Happy to be here. Um, 2025 was another remarkable year for tech. Can you hear me? Okay. >> Yeah. Go ahead. >> 2025 was another remarkable year for tech. Liberation Day tariffs in April triggered another sharp sell-off, but was ultimately the starting point for a strong rise in stocks through October as investors rotated back into AI infrastructure. Under the surface, not all tech was in the green last year. ServiceNow CEO Bill McDermott goes one-on-one with Jim Cramer

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·10:04 ·19.5K views · 2026-01-29
OpenAI Anthropic Earnings
ServiceNow reported exceptionally strong financial results and announced a significant $5 billion share buyback, directly addressing and refuting investor concerns that generative AI will disrupt enterprise software.
ServiceNow reported exceptionally strong financial results and announced a significant $5 billion share buyback, directly addressing and refuting investor concerns that generative AI will disrupt enterprise software. Management emphasizes that AI is a core enabler integrated...

TL;DR

ServiceNow reported exceptionally strong financial results and announced a significant $5 billion share buyback, directly addressing and refuting investor concerns that generative AI will disrupt enterprise software. Management emphasizes that AI is a core enabler integrated into their unique platform, driving business process reinvention and expanding market opportunity.

Market Headwinds & ServiceNow's Standout Performance

  • Enterprise software stocks are facing significant multiple compression, primarily driven by Wall Street's fear that generative AI platforms will displace them.
      • ServiceNow's stock is down over 45% from its peak one year ago, despite solid underlying performance.
      • The market has "pummeled" these stocks through multiple compression, even when earnings are "fine."
  • ServiceNow delivered a robust financial performance, exceeding Q4 and full-year expectations.
      • The company is recognized as the fastest in enterprise software to organically reach $1 billion, $5 billion, $10 billion, and now $15 billion+ in revenue.
      • It operates at the "Rule of 55" (growing revenue >20% with >35% free cash flow margin) and has upped its guidance to the "Rule of 57" for the current year.

AI as an Accelerator, Not a Threat

  • Management directly challenges the thesis that AI is "eating software", asserting it is "not true" and that the market is moving towards deeper integration.
      • ServiceNow already has strategic partnerships with leading AI providers, including OpenAI and Anthropic.
      • Dario from Anthropic highlights the "meaningful difference between giving enterprises access to an AI model and building that model into workflows where real decisions are made," which is ServiceNow's core strength.
  • ServiceNow acts as a "great consolidator," leveraging AI to reinvent business processes rather than being disrupted by it.
      • The platform is designed to work harmoniously with language models, hyperscalers, and data lakes to compose "a masterpiece" for business transformation.
      • AI's value is realized by connecting it to business applications, not through infrastructure alone, which is ServiceNow's focus.
  • Strategic acquisitions enhance the platform's AI-driven capabilities and critical functions.
      • Moveworks provides an "Agentic AI user experience" for search, request fulfillment, and actions across any data source.
      • Armis significantly bolsters security offerings, critical in a world facing $1 trillion/month in cybercrime.
          • Armis provides real-time, agentless discovery and classification of all assets (IT, OT, medical, industrial), reducing exposure before incidents.
          • ServiceNow's security business is already growing at 100% year over year.

Expanding Market & Strategic Growth Initiatives

  • ServiceNow is experiencing significant user growth and has a vast untapped market.
      • Monthly Active Users (MAU) are increasing by 25% year-over-year.
      • The Total Addressable Market (TAM) has grown from $90 billion in 2019 to $600 billion, with 1.3 billion potential seats still available.
  • New revenue vectors and a flexible pricing model are driving continued expansion.
      • Customers are now purchasing additional Assist AI packs as they consume tokens beyond their initial licensing agreements.
      • A hybrid pricing model, based on both seats and consumption, allows the company to meet customer needs effectively.
  • The platform is expanding across all business functions and geographies, with customers consuming applications in every department.

Commitment to Shareholder Value

  • ServiceNow's management demonstrates profound confidence in the company's valuation and future prospects.
      • A $5 billion share repurchase program was announced, including an immediate $2 billion accelerated repurchase.
      • CEO Bill McDermott has "bet my personal comp all on the stock," reinforcing strong conviction.
  • The company differentiates itself as "one of one" in the enterprise, distinct from "functional or feature companies" that might be vulnerable to AI.
      • ServiceNow positions itself as the sole platform capable of reinventing business processes on the fly using AI.
  • Management expects a multiple re-rating to reflect these unique results and the company's strong market position.

Notable Quotes

"ENTERPRISE SOFTWARE COHORT HAS JUST BEEN A HOUSE OF PAIN BECAUSE WALL STREET'S TERRIFIED THEY'RE GOING TO BE STEAMROLLED BY GENERATIVE AI PLATFORMS THAT ARE VERY GOOD AT WRITING CODE, BUT WE REALLY HAVEN'T SEEN ALL THAT MUCH IMPACT IN THE ACTUAL NUMBERS FOR THESE COMPANIES." - on the prevailing market sentiment impacting enterprise software. "There's a meaningful difference between giving enterprises access to an AI model and building that model into workflows where real decisions are made." - Dario, Anthropic (quoted by Bill McDermott), on effective AI integration in the enterprise. "AI DOESN'T PAY OFF BY INFRASTRUCTURE ALONE. YOU GOT TO CONNECT IT TO BUSINESS APPLICATIONS WHERE COMPANIES CAN GET THE VALUE. THAT'S WHERE WE'RE PLAYING." - Bill McDermott, on how ServiceNow creates real business value from AI. "WE ARE ONE OF ONE IN THE ENTERPRISE. IT'S THE ONLY PLATFORM THAT CAN REINVENT BUSINESS PROCESSES ON THE FLY, USING AI AND ALL THE TOOLS THAT CUSTOMERS ARE DESERVING OF." - Bill McDermott, on ServiceNow's unique and indispensable position in the market.

The Wartime CEO Vlad Tenev of Robinhood

Sequoia Capital(130.0K) ·43:25 ·89.2K views · 2026-01-29
Sequoia Keynote
Vlad Tenev, CEO of Robinhood, is renowned for his unflappable leadership, consistently making clear, long-term decisions amidst intense crises like the GameStop frenzy and severe market downturns.
Vlad Tenev, CEO of Robinhood, is renowned for his unflappable leadership, consistently making clear, long-term decisions amidst intense crises like the GameStop frenzy and severe market downturns. His leadership style emphasizes a constant "wartime" urgency, methodical product...

TL;DR

Vlad Tenev, CEO of Robinhood, is renowned for his unflappable leadership, consistently making clear, long-term decisions amidst intense crises like the GameStop frenzy and severe market downturns. His leadership style emphasizes a constant "wartime" urgency, methodical product improvement to rebuild trust, and adaptable strategic planning to maintain high velocity and deep customer connection at scale.

Crisis Leadership & Resilience

  • Unflappable leadership through intense, complex crises:
      • Navigated the GameStop trading frenzy (2021), including testifying before Congress and raising billions overnight ($3 billion by end of day).
      • Managed a full-day outage during the COVID-19 pandemic.
      • Steered the company through the 2022 market crash, where Robinhood's stock plummeted over 90% (from $80s to below $6).
      • Handled the 2023 SVB crisis.
  • Unique approach under pressure:
      • While most CEOs panic, Vlad "gets clarity" and makes methodical decisions with a long-term approach.
      • Sequoia partner Andrew described him as "the steadiest hand he's ever met."
  • Worst crisis identified as the 2022 market downturn:
      • Considered more extreme than acute events like GameStop due to its "slow burn" nature, lasting 400-500 days.
      • The fundamental business model, built for a zero-interest rate environment, was challenged, requiring significant adaptation.
      • Used the crisis as an opportunity to diversify the business, unlike companies without such "levers."
  • Immediate crisis response actions:
      • Quickly mobilizes "the best people," ensuring they have all necessary resources.
      • CEO's specific roles: public communication of complex situations and securing emergency funding.

Rebuilding Trust and Customer Focus

  • Trust is easily lost, but hard-won:
      • NPS (Net Promoter Score) plummeted during public incidents like the COVID outage and GameStop.
      • Trust gains are "slower and more gradual than the losses" – it took HubSpot about a year and a half to recover NPS after an outage.
  • Strategy for rebuilding trust emphasizes consistent improvement:
      • No "magic bullets" like blog posts or gestures can instantly restore trust.
      • Focus on "just improving your products over time" and minimizing repeated mistakes.
      • Gradual, persistent improvement over many years compounds to rebuild and surpass previous trust levels.
  • Maintaining customer connection at scale (15,000 employees):
      • Encourages all executives and leadership to conduct customer support calls and engage in UX research.
      • Vlad personally undertakes customer support tickets (phone/chat) at least once a year, gaining direct, high-signal feedback.
      • Emphasizes conducting UX research through direct customer engagement, including home visits, to understand product usage and needs deeply.

Culture, Urgency, and Planning

  • "Always wartime" mentality as a default:
      • Believes in maintaining a constant state of urgency, ideally "internally created" to maintain control and drive.
      • Perceives "peacetime" moments as signs of insufficient aggression in execution.
  • Sustaining speed and urgency as the company scales:
      • Requires explicit commitment to speed, clear measurement of velocity, and consistent communication of its importance.
      • Strategies involve analyzing differences between fast-moving and slower teams to identify and replicate best practices.
  • Rejecting the urgency-quality tradeoff:
      • Demands both high speed *and* high quality, stating, "you have to move fast, and I don't want any corners cut."
      • Compares it to top engineers who write fast, high-quality code without bugs.
      • Refers to accepting tradeoffs as the "tyranny of 'or'" and strives for "and."
  • Lean and adaptable strategic planning:
      • Minimizes planning time to "a couple of days" rather than multi-week "waterfall" processes.
      • Maintains a 10-year long-term vision, which is revisited annually, ensuring the core mission remains consistent while the path clarifies.
      • Emphasizes "working backward" from the 10-year vision, investing in long-term arcs *now* despite delayed returns.
      • Conducts an annual "diff" to compare prior year's plan with actual outcomes and recalibrate.
      • Financial planning is tightly managed by a limited group for public company predictability.
      • No intentional "slack" in the system: Reprioritizes in real-time when significant new information or opportunities emerge (e.g., CFTC approval for election contracts).
  • Product launch events as strategic forcing functions:
      • Uses highly publicized product events (e.g., "Gold event," "Hood Summit") with specific deadlines to structure product development, drive urgency, and foster community.

CEO Qualities & Advice for Founders

  • Meritocratic approach to strategy:
      • Engages approximately 150 top-performing individuals, including individual contributors and junior staff, in strategic vision discussions, not solely senior executives.
  • Perspective on Co-CEOs:
      • Sees increasing prevalence and benefits, especially in early stages, allowing aggressive partnership without excessive "decision overhead."
      • Cites examples like Netflix, Oracle, and Salesforce that have utilized co-CEO models.
  • Advice for aspiring CEOs:
      • Disregard superficial advice: Avoid relying on "Twitter and gauging how to run your company."
      • Prioritize real-world feedback: Focus on identifying what *needs* to be delivered and work backward, iterating based on direct customer feedback and internal company functioning.
      • Reason by first principles: Advocates for focusing on core needs rather than merely reasoning by analogy or copying others' strategies.

Notable Quotes

"You're woken up at 5:00 a.m. by a telephone call saying you need to raise $3 billion by the end of the day or you're toast. It's a CEO's worst nightmare." - on extreme financial pressure during crisis. "While most CEOs panic under pressure, Vlad gets clarity. While others make reactive decisions, he builds methodically with a long-term approach." - on his distinctive leadership style. "It's easy to lose trust. It's very hard to build it back... But really what it is is just improving your products over time." - on the fundamental strategy for rebuilding customer trust. "My experience is you have to demand both. It's like you have to move fast, and I don't want any corners cut... It's not really a tradeoff." - on integrating speed and quality in execution. "I think it's always wartime. Ideally, the wartime is internally created because then it's under your control." - on cultivating a persistent state of urgency and internal drive.
At the GameStop situation, for instance, the things I had to do were figure out how we communicate to the public what was going on with this very complex situation and then also raise billions of dollars overnight. You're woken up at 5:00 a.m. by a telephone call saying you need to raise $3 billion by the end of the day or you're toast. It's a CEO's worst nightmare. Vlad, the CEO of Robin Hood, lived through this story, came out the other side stronger than ever. Vlad has become legendary for his unflappable leadership through some of the most intense crises in startup history. Robin Hood is now a financial services juggernaut, but Vlad's journey was a roller coaster from the start. From the GameStop trading frenzy that made headlines worldwide and landed him testifying before Congress, to a full day outage during the COVID pandemic, to navigating the 2022 market crash that saw their stock price plummet by more than 90%. Vlad has been on a 4D CEO roller coaster. While most CEOs panic under pressure, Vlad gets clarity. While others make reactive decisions, he builds methodically with a long-term approach. Today, you'll hear how to stay calm and make clear decisions when your company is in crisis mode, why wartime mentality should be your default and not the exception, the art of rebuilding trust after public failures, how to maintain speed and urgency as you scale to thousands of employees, and how to balance long-term vision with short-term agility. If you're a founder trying to build resilience into yourself or your company, or if you want to understand what separates good CEOs from legendary ones, keep listening. Vlad, good to see you again. Good to see you. My partner at Sequoia, Andrew, said of you, "You're the steadiest hand he's ever met." What do you think he meant by that? Maybe compared to him, he can be a little bit jittery. A bit of a jittery guy, always nervous, "when am I going to get the next deal?" But I love Andrew. I think what he probably meant was, he's seen Robin Hood go through some downs, not just ups. And maybe a lot of people would have just called it in and given up, but we persevered and we worked through it. And now I believe we're a much stronger company than we've ever been. And I think that's an important quality of a founder. It's really just about how you handle the situation when things are maybe unexpectedly getting worse, and you have to respond and make changes. And also unexpectedly getting better, because that can also be an opportunity. Things change, maybe your business gets some tailwinds, and you also have to be in a position to capitalize on that without being distracted. I think maybe by steadiness he refers to being able to capture things when things are going in our favor, and also not getting too discouraged and being able to pivot strategically when things are not. I think so too. When I look back at HubSpot, I used to describe HubSpot as a roller coaster. You guys are more like a 4D roller coaster where you're upside down the whole time. There's been a lot of those are my favorite ones. And you're in one, and just to name a few, every year for a while, you had one. The beginning of COVID, you guys had that big outage for a whole day. 2021, of course, all the GameStop stuff. They make a movie about you. You're testifying in front of Congress. 2022, the economy shifted. You lost half your users. Pivoted the whole darn company. 2023, the SVB stuff was pretty bad. Which was the worst? And can you talk a little bit about what it was like going through it and how your body reacted and how you engaged with the company and the market when that was going on, because most people freak out. A lot of people, I think, when they talk about our crises, they probably would say GameStop because that was the most public, and I think each one tends to drown out the previous one. So before GameStop, there was a lot of chatter about the outage right at the beginning of the COVID pandemic that you mentioned. Those things, I think in hindsight, weren't that bad to navigate through because it was an acute moment. The worst of it is right at the beginning, and then once you make it through and you're in the clear, it's cleanup. You've probably been through some of those yourself. I'd say 2022, the market turning, was more extreme for me because that was a slow burn. First, there was signaling by the Fed that interest rates were going to go up because inflation was looking bad. So, it started out as this general signaling, but the market got it right. And I would say as one of the recently IPO growth companies of that era, end of 2021, we got hit first because when people start taking these macro things into effect and adjusting their portfolios, they take risky assets off first. And so you have crypto for those that are interested in that, and recently IPOed growth stocks are in that bucket. And we were among the most recent IPOs. We just snuck in right at the end of that window, in hindsight, without realizing it. I think that if we had waited a couple more months, we probably wouldn't have been able to go public until maybe 2024. And so, it was just a slow burn. Stock price consistently and steadily decreasing from a high of, I think, in the 80s right after our IPO to below $8, maybe even below $6 would have been our all-time low. We were trading below cash, and that was tough because you have employees wondering what's going on. The street just turned very bearish. But behind that was a business whose fundamentals did work very, very well in a zero interest rate environment where there was a lot of trading, but in a high interest rate environment, we weren't really built to succeed in that environment. And I took a little bit of solace in the fact that I felt like we had a lot of levers. We ultimately used that as an opportunity to diversify the business, and a lot of other companies didn't have those levers. So if you were a mortgage company, you basically had to batten down the hatches and figure out how to make it through until rates went back down again, which still hasn't really happened. So I think those companies had a tough situation, but it was painful throughout, and that was more like an "each day is a little bit worse than the last, and there's a lot of challenges" situation, and that goes on for 400 to 500 days at least before you start seeing some of the fruits of the labor that ended up turning things around. And inside your body, you get a call probably at 2:00 in the morning about the GameStop thing. What's going on in your head? Do you get clarity? Do you get confusion? Do you bring your team together? Do you remember when you got the call? Yes. You go through a few things, right? Are the best people awake? Are they in need of anything? And of course, the answer to the first one is no. You have to make sure you get them in the room, get them right away, and you have to know who the best people are, which I think requires being intimately familiar with the details. And if they need anything, we drop everything and make sure they have those things. And then are there things that I need to do that perhaps only I can do in that situation? At the GameStop situation, the things that I had to do were figure out how we communicate to the public what was going on with this very complex situation and then also raise billions of dollars overnight. Yeah. So then, that was actually kind of nice because those were pretty clear goals. My guess is when those things are happening and you gather the team at 5:00 in the morning on a Zoom, I would imagine people are pretty panicked, and I guess what Andrew meant is in the face of big adversity and some weird things going down that you didn't expect, you get clarity and you're very clear with your team and you calm them down. I think that's the opposite reaction of 99% of humanity. Yeah. And I think that's part of your special sauce. Yeah, I think that's right. And I wouldn't oversell it too much. I mean, I don't know if I always make the right decisions in that moment, but probably the variance between a decision under fire and one where we're just in a normal meeting is a little bit lower. At HubSpot, we had times of relative peace and some times of war. I enjoyed the wartime. Do you think about it like that or is it always wartime or how do you think about that? I think it's always wartime. Yeah. Ideally, the wartime is internally created because then it's under your control. You're not subject to some other people's wars that are being imposed on you. I think if you get into, "oh, this is peacetime, things are going smoothly," in hindsight, I've had moments where I'm sitting around the table with my co-founder or my executive team, and it's like, "ah, there's really not too many problems going on right now." And at the time, it felt good, but in hindsight, I was like, we probably weren't executing aggressively enough. I see. And in all of these cases, you tested your customers' trust in you, and you lost a ton of trust in all those cases, but built it back. Can you talk about how you thought about building that back? How do you build back trust? What metrics are you looking at? You're obsessed with NPS, or it's easy to lose trust. It's very hard to build it back, and you've done it multiple times here. What advice do you have for others on this? Yeah, we do look at NPS, and NPS definitely took a dive during the COVID times, during GameStop in particular. You see it in these public incidents, whether it's the outages or the GameStop incident, and it's true that it can go from very high to low practically overnight, but the reverse is rare, right? I mean, maybe if you have a really big announcement that people get excited about, you'll gain five points of NPS, but the gains are always slower and more gradual than the losses. I didn't really believe it when I heard it that trust is easily lost and hard gained, but I think it's true in the data. So, we look for magic bullets, like can we say these magic words or issue a blog post and make everything okay or come up with some gesture? But really what it is is just improving your products over time. And if you keep doing good things, and invariably there will be some mistakes along the way for sure, but minimize those mistakes. Make sure you don't do the same mistake over and over again. I think that's a big killer. Then you see that gradual increase, and over many, many years that compounds, and you could be back at all-time highs. I think you've seen it happening with some of the great companies. Nike had their challenges back in the early 90s, Apple certainly had their challenges. Domino's Pizza, or was it Papa John's? One of the two, I think it was a famous trust case study. The pepperoni wasn't that good, but they really just fixed it. So it can happen. So in all those cases, trust took a hit. NPS went way down. In your head, are you thinking, "in 12 months, I can get it back up to where it is," or "in 6 months?" How do you think about that? At HubSpot, we had an outage in 2019 on the last day of the quarter, and we lost a lot of customers. Our NPS plummeted, and it was about a year and a half before the NPS caught back up to where it was. Yeah, I think it can take years. Of course, we have goals. We try to minimize it, but there's really no better way that I found than just improving the other things, other than maybe the acute brand episode that you had. I don't think you can counteract that. But if you just keep making your business better, then the effects of any individual crisis over time will fade. But then the effects of improving your product are persistent, those will continue. Okay. I spend a lot of time coaching about 20 different scale-up CEOs. A lot of the companies, as they're scaling, get frustrated that the intensity in the company goes down, the tempo goes down. My sense of you guys is the tempo has stayed high, and you're very quick. CFTC got approved as a trading platform; within a couple weeks, you were live with that system. What advice do you have for founders that are scaling that don't want to turn into a big, slow company? I think the main thing is you actually have to care about it. A lot of these people are sitting around the table saying, "gosh, we're moving a lot slower now." They complain about it, but do they really care enough to fix it? I think that you have to talk about it not just to the public or to your friends, but also to your team. You have to measure it. So if you say, "we want to move faster, we want to have high velocity, we want to ship more," how do you do that? And how do you know that you've got that? So I think measurement is important. You have to communicate that it's important. You have to measure it. You have to have strategies for how to improve it, and that involves looking at areas where maybe it's not doing so well and comparing it with the areas where you are moving a little bit faster and seeing what those folks are doing over there that you should incorporate. There was always a struggle in my mind at HubSpot; we'd roll something out and some things take a while, and a tension in my mind between urgency and patience on stuff. Do you feel that tension in your head, like you've got to be patient, this is going to take time, it's a big initiative, it's going to take a while, versus always pressing and creating urgency on everything? I think there's a difference between the tactical and the strategic urgency. I think that if you have a lot of uncertainty in what the right thing to do is, it makes sense to gather more information. When the right thing to do is clear and everyone is aligned, at that point, you have to move through with maximal urgency. And I think a lot of times what can be a trap is an urgency-quality tradeoff. If we move fast, we'll end up having to cut corners. My experience is you have to demand both. It's like you have to move fast, and I don't want any corners cut. If you look at a really good engineer, the best engineers move the fastest and also have high-quality code with no bugs. It's not really a tradeoff. It's like you can find it within one person. And then if you get a team of those people together and you create the right incentive structures for you to have an incredibly high density of great people like that, then I think you don't have that tradeoff. For some people, moving faster does lead to more mistakes, but not for everyone. I think for the best people, it's not true. I used to call that the tyranny of "or." Everyone would bring me "or," and I'd be like, "how about an 'and' just once? Let's do an 'and'." Yeah. And they also say if you need something done, give it to your busiest person, right? So there's some counterintuitive advice there where great people just find a way. I agree. Okay. One of the hacks that some of these founders are doing is requiring people to be in the office seven days a week. What's your take on that? What I noticed, my generation, we followed the Steve Jobs playbook. It feels like this generation follows the Elon playbook. And both playbooks are great. Elon's playbook is a little bit like you're working 180 hours a week. You're in the office. You're sleeping in the office. Yeah. And a hack is a minimum of the 996, 9 to 9, 6 days a week, but a lot of them are 7 days a week. And it doesn't scale, but I'm seeing them push it beyond 100, 200 employees. It's interesting. Any take on that? That's an extreme approach. It works for some people. The tradeoff is you're not going to get people that are sticking around the company very long, because it's hard to work 7 days a week for 10 years if you want to have a girlfriend or children that you see. So there's an extreme for sure. The right balance is some number greater than 40 but perhaps less than 180. And I'm not exactly sure what the number is. I mean, obviously, we like to push it and work hard here and hold ourselves to a high standard. Okay. Related to this. I met with a founder yesterday of a red-hot AI company, and he showed me his phone, and he just showed me all the Slack messages, all the email messages, all the texts, all the WhatsApp, and he said, "since we closed our round and we're getting a little bit famous here, I'm completely overcome and inundated with just inbound stuff, and I spend all my day trying to get through that." And he said, "Brian, how did you manage the chaos?" Yeah, I thought that was a good question. I'm curious how you'd answer that. There's a finite amount of time and a finite number of those messages that probably deserve and benefit from the founder's attention and decision-making ability. It's tricky because you want to see them. Sometimes automatic delegation doesn't work because someone's not going to know how to handle it, but you don't want to spend all your time processing them. So, I think the best way is to just have people around you, and if you don't add them one at a time deliberately, that you can offload the majority of that work to. You seem to be very good at aligning your vectors over there. How do you decide which bets to play? How do you keep your vectors aligned? How often do you rejigger the priorities? What's your planning look like? How does all that work as you're scaling it up? I think it depends a lot on what's going on, like market conditions, what we see doing really well in the business, what we see that we could devote more resources to. I think a lot of times you're beholden to a structure, annual planning or quarterly planning. Monthly Business Reviews, Quarterly Business Reviews. There are these structures that make it so that you either have to plan when you don't really need to plan, or you're waiting too long to respond to things because maybe something happened, but you just had your quarterly business review where you fixed the plan, and it's locked in, and it's this heavyweight thing. My push has been to just minimize the time spent planning. Minimize it to a couple of days. That's the goal. Rather than having it be this multi-week waterfall thing, can we do all our planning in less than a week, and then just keep pushing on that until you reach a point where it's obviously too little, and then we can readjust. How does this cycle work with your fiscal year? How do you actually do it? Usually, what we do is we have our long-term vision that goes out 10 years. How often do you change that? I think from time to time, when something major changes, like we have a very clear epiphany or some data from some projects that are at some level of maturation, there can be a pretty big change. So I had a pretty big one a couple years ago, and it then led to an overhaul of our vision and how we communicated. And I think the core of it was the same throughout the entire life of the company. We knew where we wanted to get to, which is just everyone using Robin Hood for all of their financial needs. The path became more clear. We got better at articulating it. And it culminated in an investor event that we held in New York in December of last year where we actually invited our public company analysts, retail analysts as well, this community of folks on YouTube that analyzes companies. And I don't know if you've come across this issue with them, but I think the first approximation to a 10-year vision is you just put all the things that you want to do that are in your mind right now onto a timeline. And what ends up happening is all the exciting initiatives are years one and two. And then as you get further along and you get to year 10, you look at this thing and you're like, "Wow, all of my low priority initiatives are just there." So I think in a naive approximation, it doesn't actually reflect the reality. And the reality is the 10-year vision you should work backwards from. That should be really, really big. And you're not going to work on those things nine years from now. You actually have to work on them now. And they have to build upon one another so that you actually get to where you think you should be in 10 years. I like to think of them as arcs. And you have an arc that you're in right now, and then you have a medium-term one, and then we have our 10-year arc, which is going to feel slow at first, but eventually is just going to take over the entire business. And you have to invest in that 10-year arc right now, knowing that the ultimate return is going to be big but actually delayed. And I think you have to communicate that to the company and to the street in our case and make sure that they understand the investments. I think Meta actually did a good job of this.
